SENP5

SENP5 in Prostate Cancer

SENP5 (Sentrin-specific protease 5) is a member of the SUMO-specific protease family, which plays a crucial role in the post-translational modification of proteins through deSUMOylation. This process is important for regulating various cellular functions, including transcription, cell cycle progression, and apoptosis. The involvement of SENP5 in cancer, including prostate cancer, has been a subject of interest due to its potential role in tumorigenesis.

Role of SENP5 in Cancer

  1. Oncogenic Properties: SENP5 has been implicated as an oncogene in several cancer types. Studies have shown that overexpression of SENP5 can promote cell proliferation and inhibit apoptosis, contributing to tumor growth and survival. For instance, in gastric cancer, SENP5 was found to be significantly upregulated in tumor tissues compared to normal tissues, suggesting a similar potential role in other cancers, including prostate cancer[1][2].

  2. SUMOylation Regulation: SENP5 is involved in the regulation of SUMOylation, a process that modifies proteins and influences their function and stability. This modification is particularly relevant for the androgen receptor (AR), which is critical in prostate cancer. Although specific studies directly linking SENP5 to AR activity in prostate cancer are limited, its role as a SUMO-specific protease suggests it may influence AR-mediated transcriptional activity by modulating the SUMOylation state of AR[3].

  3. Cell Cycle and Apoptosis: Research indicates that SENP5 is essential for maintaining mitochondrial function and cytokinesis, which are vital for cell division and survival. Knockdown studies have demonstrated that reducing SENP5 levels can lead to increased SUMO conjugates and defects in cell morphology, ultimately affecting cell growth and promoting apoptosis[2]. These effects could be particularly significant in the context of prostate cancer, where cell proliferation and survival are critical for tumor progression.

Implications for Prostate Cancer Research

  • Potential Biomarker: Given its role in cell growth and survival, SENP5 could serve as a potential biomarker for aggressive prostate cancer or as a therapeutic target. Its expression levels may correlate with disease progression or response to therapies.

  • Target for Therapy: Targeting SENP5 or its pathways could provide new avenues for treatment strategies aimed at inhibiting tumor growth or enhancing the efficacy of existing therapies.

Conclusion

While direct evidence linking SENP5 specifically to prostate cancer remains limited, its established roles in other cancers and its regulatory functions related to the androgen receptor suggest that it may play a significant role in prostate cancer biology. Further research is needed to clarify its specific contributions and potential as a therapeutic target or biomarker within this context.

SLC38A1

SLC38A1 in Prostate Cancer

SLC38A1 (Solute Carrier Family 38 Member 1) is a gene that encodes a sodium-dependent neutral amino acid transporter. Its role in prostate cancer has been explored in the context of tumor biology and progression, although specific studies focusing solely on SLC38A1 in prostate cancer are limited.

Expression and Function

  1. Amino Acid Transport: SLC38A1 is primarily involved in the transport of amino acids across cell membranes, which is crucial for various cellular processes, including protein synthesis and energy metabolism. In cancer cells, altered amino acid metabolism is often observed, supporting rapid growth and proliferation.

  2. Potential Role in Tumorigenesis: While direct evidence linking SLC38A1 to prostate cancer progression is sparse, its involvement in amino acid transport suggests it may play a role in the metabolic adaptations of prostate cancer cells. Tumors often exhibit increased reliance on specific nutrients, including amino acids, to sustain their growth.

Clinical Relevance

  • Fusion Genes and Prognosis: In a broader context of prostate cancer research, certain fusion genes have been identified that correlate with disease progression. For instance, studies have shown that patients with specific gene fusions, including those involving SLC45A2 and AMACR, have a higher risk of recurrence and metastasis after treatment[1]. Although SLC38A1 itself is not mentioned as a fusion gene, understanding its expression dynamics could provide insights into metabolic vulnerabilities in prostate cancer.

  • Biomarker Potential: Given its function in amino acid transport, SLC38A1 could potentially serve as a biomarker for assessing metabolic changes in prostate cancer. Alterations in its expression might reflect the aggressive nature of tumors or their response to therapies.

Conclusion

While research specifically targeting SLC38A1’s role in prostate cancer is limited, its function as an amino acid transporter positions it as a potentially important player in the metabolic landscape of tumors. Further investigation into its expression patterns and functional implications could enhance our understanding of prostate cancer biology and contribute to the development of novel therapeutic strategies.

RCCD1

RCCD1 in Prostate Cancer

RCCD1 (Regulator of Chromosome Condensation 1) is a gene that has been implicated in various cellular processes, including cell cycle regulation and chromosomal stability. Its specific role in prostate cancer is still being elucidated, but emerging evidence suggests it may contribute to tumorigenesis and cancer progression.

Expression and Potential Role

  1. Cell Cycle Regulation: RCCD1 is known to play a role in the regulation of the cell cycle and chromosomal organization. Dysregulation of these processes can lead to genomic instability, a hallmark of cancer, including prostate cancer.

  2. Association with Tumor Characteristics: While direct studies linking RCCD1 expression to prostate cancer outcomes are limited, genes involved in chromosomal stability often correlate with aggressive tumor characteristics. This suggests that RCCD1 may be involved in the progression of prostate cancer or its response to therapies.

Genetic Studies

  • Genome-Wide Association Studies (GWAS): Recent large-scale GWAS have identified numerous risk variants associated with prostate cancer, some of which may involve pathways related to RCCD1. Although RCCD1 itself was not specifically highlighted in these studies, the broader context of chromosomal regulation indicates its potential relevance in prostate cancer susceptibility and progression.

Clinical Implications

  • Biomarker Potential: Given its involvement in cell cycle regulation, RCCD1 could serve as a potential biomarker for assessing tumor aggressiveness or predicting treatment responses in prostate cancer patients. Further research is necessary to validate this potential.

Conclusion

RCCD1’s role in prostate cancer remains an area of active investigation. Its involvement in cell cycle regulation and chromosomal stability suggests it could play a significant part in the biology of prostate tumors. Ongoing research may clarify its specific contributions and potential as a therapeutic target or biomarker in prostate cancer management.

CHD3

CHD3 in Prostate Cancer

CHD3 (Chromodomain Helicase DNA Binding Protein 3) is a member of the CHD family of proteins, which are involved in chromatin remodeling and gene regulation. While specific studies focusing solely on CHD3 in prostate cancer are limited, insights can be drawn from its general role within the CHD family and its potential implications in cancer biology.

Role in Chromatin Remodeling

  1. Nucleosome Remodeling: CHD3 is part of the NuRD (nucleosome remodeling and deacetylase) complex, which plays a critical role in chromatin remodeling by altering nucleosome positioning and modifying histones. This function is essential for regulating gene expression, particularly in processes related to cell proliferation and differentiation[2][4].

  2. Tumorigenesis: The NuRD complex, including CHD3, has been implicated in various cancers through its involvement in epithelial-to-mesenchymal transition (EMT) and metastasis. Such processes are crucial for cancer progression, including prostate cancer[2].

Implications in Prostate Cancer

  1. Oncogenic Potential: Although direct evidence linking CHD3 to prostate cancer specifically is sparse, its role as a chromatin remodeler suggests that it may influence pathways relevant to oncogenesis. For instance, aberrations in chromatin remodeling can lead to the activation of oncogenes or silencing of tumor suppressor genes, contributing to cancer development[4][5].

  2. Expression Patterns: Bioinformatics analyses have shown that members of the CHD family, including CHD3, exhibit distinct expression patterns across various cancer types. While higher expression levels of some CHD proteins correlate with poor prognosis in certain cancers, the specific relationship between CHD3 expression and clinical outcomes in prostate cancer requires further investigation[4].

Conclusion

The role of CHD3 in prostate cancer remains an area needing more focused research. Its involvement in chromatin remodeling through the NuRD complex indicates potential pathways through which it could affect tumorigenesis and cancer progression. Understanding the functional dynamics of CHD3 could provide valuable insights into its contributions to prostate cancer biology and potential therapeutic targets.

ARMC1

ARMC1 in Prostate Cancer

ARMC1 (Armadillo Repeat Containing 1) is a gene that has been studied primarily in the context of various cancers, including breast cancer, but its specific role in prostate cancer is less well-defined. Here are some insights regarding ARMC1’s potential implications in prostate cancer based on available research:

Expression and Prognostic Significance

  1. Expression Levels: While direct studies on ARMC1 expression in prostate cancer are limited, it is known that ARMC1 expression is often elevated in various tumor types compared to normal tissues. This pattern suggests that ARMC1 may also be overexpressed in prostate cancer, similar to its behavior in other malignancies like breast cancer[1].

  2. Prognostic Marker: In breast cancer, higher levels of ARMC1 have been associated with poorer survival outcomes, indicating its potential as a prognostic marker. Although specific data for prostate cancer are not extensively documented, the trend of increased expression correlating with adverse clinical outcomes could imply a similar role in prostate cancer[1].

Potential Mechanisms

  1. Cell Cycle Regulation: Functional enrichment analyses indicate that ARMC1 may be involved in regulating the cell cycle, which is a critical aspect of cancer progression. Dysregulation of cell cycle proteins can lead to uncontrolled cell proliferation, a hallmark of cancer[1].

  2. Chromatin Regulation and Signaling Pathways: The role of ARMC1 in chromatin remodeling and its interactions with various signaling pathways could influence prostate cancer development and progression. Chromatin modifications are crucial for the regulation of gene expression, and any aberration may contribute to oncogenesis[2][4].

Conclusion

While specific studies focusing on ARMC1 in prostate cancer are scarce, its established roles in other cancers suggest it may also have significant implications in prostate cancer biology. Increased expression levels and potential involvement in cell cycle regulation highlight the need for further research to clarify ARMC1’s role as a biomarker and therapeutic target in prostate cancer. Understanding these dynamics could provide valuable insights into the management and treatment of this disease.

PIM1

PIM1 in Prostate Cancer

PIM1 (Proviral Integration site for Moloney murine leukemia virus 1) is a serine/threonine kinase that has garnered attention in the study of prostate cancer due to its role in tumorigenesis, therapy resistance, and cellular proliferation. Here are key points regarding the involvement of PIM1 in prostate cancer:

Expression and Role in Tumorigenesis

  1. Overexpression in Prostate Cancer: PIM1 is frequently overexpressed in prostate cancer tissues, particularly in high-grade prostate intraepithelial neoplasia and malignant tumors compared to normal prostatic tissue. This overexpression correlates with poor therapeutic outcomes[1][3].

  2. Mechanisms of Action: PIM1 promotes cancer cell survival by inhibiting apoptosis and enhancing cell cycle progression. It can phosphorylate various substrates, including the androgen receptor (AR), which affects AR degradation and function, particularly under low androgen conditions. This mechanism implicates PIM1 in the development of castration-resistant prostate cancer[1][2].

Impact on Therapy Resistance

  1. Docetaxel Resistance: Elevated levels of PIM1 have been linked to resistance against docetaxel, a common chemotherapy drug used in advanced prostate cancer. Studies indicate that reducing PIM1 levels can sensitize prostate cancer cells to docetaxel treatment, suggesting that targeting PIM1 may enhance the efficacy of existing therapies[1][5].

  2. Potential Therapeutic Target: Given its significant role in promoting tumor growth and therapy resistance, PIM1 is considered a promising target for novel cancer therapies. Several small-molecule inhibitors targeting PIM kinases are currently undergoing clinical trials[2][4].

Influence on Cellular Dynamics

  1. Lipid Droplet Accumulation: Recent studies have identified PIM1 as a driver of lipid droplet accumulation in prostate cancer cells, which supports cell proliferation during nutrient stress. This accumulation is linked to enhanced survival and metabolic adaptation of cancer cells[2].

  2. Cell Motility and Metastasis: PIM1 has been shown to enhance the motility of prostate cancer cells by phosphorylating actin capping proteins, thereby promoting cell adhesion and migration. This action contributes to the invasive and metastatic potential of prostate tumors[4].

Conclusion

PIM1 plays a multifaceted role in the progression of prostate cancer through mechanisms involving cell survival, therapy resistance, and enhanced motility. Its overexpression is associated with aggressive tumor behavior and poor treatment outcomes, making it a critical focus for ongoing research aimed at developing targeted therapies for prostate cancer management.

CTBP2

CTBP2 in Prostate Cancer

CTBP2 (C-terminal binding protein 2) is a transcriptional corepressor that has been implicated in the progression of prostate cancer through its interaction with key signaling pathways, particularly those involving the androgen receptor (AR). Here are the main findings regarding CTBP2’s role in prostate cancer:

Mechanisms of Action

  1. Regulation of Androgen Receptor: CTBP2 has been identified as a modulator of AR activity, which is crucial for the development and progression of prostate cancer. It binds to AR and represses tumor suppressor genes and AR corepressors, such as NCOR and RIP140, thereby enhancing androgen-mediated gene expression [1][2]. This interaction suggests that CTBP2 plays a significant role in promoting oncogenic signaling.

  2. Genetic Variations and Susceptibility: Genetic variations within the CTBP2 gene have been associated with susceptibility to prostate cancer. Specific single nucleotide polymorphisms (SNPs) in CTBP2 correlate with increased AR-enhancer activity, linking genetic predisposition to functional outcomes in cancer [1].

Clinical Implications

  1. Prognostic Marker: High levels of CTBP2 expression are correlated with poor prognosis in prostate cancer patients. Studies have shown that silencing CTBP2 can reduce tumor growth in xenograft models, indicating its potential as a therapeutic target [1][2].

  2. Role in Tumor Growth and Migration: Functional experiments suggest that CTBP2 promotes prostate cancer cell proliferation and migration, further emphasizing its role in tumor aggressiveness [2][3].

  3. Angiogenesis: CTBP2 has also been implicated in angiogenesis within prostate tumors, suggesting that it may contribute to the tumor microenvironment and facilitate cancer progression through enhanced blood supply [5].

Therapeutic Targeting

Given its critical involvement in AR signaling and tumor progression, CTBP2 is considered a promising target for therapeutic intervention in prostate cancer. Targeting CTBP2 could potentially disrupt its oncogenic functions and improve treatment outcomes for patients with advanced disease [3][5].

Conclusion

CTBP2 is a key player in the molecular landscape of prostate cancer, influencing both the androgen signaling pathway and tumor behavior. Its association with poor prognosis and potential as a therapeutic target highlights the importance of further research into its mechanisms and roles within prostate cancer biology.

Immune Functions

LY6G6D

LY6G6D (Lymphocyte Antigen 6 Family Member G6D) plays several important roles in immune function, particularly in colorectal cancer (CRC) and neutrophil activity:

Role in Colorectal Cancer

LY6G6D has been identified as a selectively expressed antigen in colorectal cancer, particularly in the microsatellite stable (MSS) subtype[1]. Its expression in CRC has several implications for immune function:

  • Immune Evasion: LY6G6D may help regulate tumor growth and immune evasion in CRC[1].

  • T Cell Suppression: LY6G6D-positive CRC cells can inhibit T cell proliferation by expanding myeloid-derived suppressor cells (MDSCs)[2].

  • Immunosuppressive Environment: High expression of LY6G6D is associated with increased infiltration of immunosuppressive cells like regulatory T cells (Tregs) and T helper 2 (Th2) cells[3].

Neutrophil Function

LY6G6D is expressed in various myeloid cells, including neutrophils, and plays a role in their function:

  • Neutrophil Activity: It influences neutrophil infiltration, recruitment, and migration[2].

  • Immune Senescence Marker: LY6G6D downregulation is a crucial biomarker for tracking functional deterioration associated with neutrophil immune senescence[2].

  • Neutrophil Immune Functions: LY6G6D expression is closely linked to neutrophil functions such as phagocytosis, production of reactive oxygen species (ROS) and interleukin IL-1β, formation of neutrophil extracellular traps (NETs), migration, and bacterial clearance[2].

Regulation and Signaling

The immune function of LY6G6D is regulated by several pathways:

  • JAK/STAT5 Pathway: LY6G6D is activated through the JAK/STAT5 pathway in poorly immunogenic CRCs[3].

  • p38α MAPK Signaling: p38α MAPK and DNA methyltransferases mediate LY6G6D regulation[4].

  • Epigenetic Regulation: DNA methylation changes in the LY6G6D promoter are closely related to its transcript regulation and expression in different CRC subtypes[4].

In summary, LY6G6D plays a complex role in immune function, particularly in the context of colorectal cancer and neutrophil activity. Its expression can contribute to both immune evasion in cancer and normal neutrophil function, making it an interesting target for potential immunotherapies and diagnostic applications.

NINL

Based on the search results provided, NINL (Ninein-like) plays several important roles in immune function, particularly in the antiviral innate immune response:

Antiviral Immune Response

NINL is a critical component in the antiviral innate immune response:

  • Interferon Signaling: NINL knockout cells exhibit an impaired response to interferon, resulting in increased permissiveness to viral replication[1].

  • ISG Production: Cells lacking NINL show a dramatic reduction in interferon-stimulated gene (ISG) production, likely due to decreased nuclear localization of phosphorylated STAT1 following interferon treatment[1].

  • Viral Replication: In NINL knockout cells, several RNA and DNA viruses show significantly increased replication after interferon-α pretreatment compared to wild-type cells[1].

Mechanism of Action

NINL’s role in the immune response appears to be related to its function in intracellular transport:

  • Dynein Activating Adaptor: NINL is a dynein activating adaptor, which is part of the intracellular transport machinery[1].

  • JAK/STAT Signaling: NINL acts after the phosphorylation of STAT1 and STAT2 but before ISG transcription in the interferon signaling cascade[1].

Evolutionary Significance

NINL has unique evolutionary characteristics that suggest its importance in host-pathogen interactions:

  • Positive Selection: NINL has evolved under recurrent positive (diversifying) selection, particularly in its carboxy-terminal cargo-binding region[1][2].

  • Host-Virus Genetic Conflict: This evolutionary signature is characteristic of genes involved in host-virus genetic conflicts, suggesting NINL’s role in antiviral defense[2].

Viral Antagonism

NINL is also a target of viral antagonism:

  • Viral Protease Cleavage: Proteases encoded by diverse picornaviruses and coronaviruses cleave and disrupt NINL function in a host- and virus-specific manner[1][2].

In summary, NINL plays a crucial role in the antiviral immune response by facilitating interferon signaling and ISG production. Its evolutionary history and targeting by viral proteases further underscore its importance in host-pathogen interactions. The discovery of NINL’s immune function demonstrates the utility of using signatures of host-virus genetic conflicts to uncover novel components of the immune system.

ANO7

Based on the search results provided, ANO7 (Anoctamin 7) plays several important roles in prostate biology and may have implications for immune function in prostate cancer:

Expression and Localization

  • ANO7 is a calcium2+-dependent chloride ion channel protein[1].
  • Its expression is restricted to prostate epithelial cells[1].
  • ANO7 is strongly expressed in normal prostate glandular cells, particularly at the apical pole and lateral surfaces of epithelial cells[1].

Role in Prostate Cancer

ANO7 expression appears to be altered in prostate cancer:

  • ANO7 is often less abundant in cancer cells compared to normal prostate tissue[1].
  • Reduced ANO7 staining is tightly linked to adverse tumor features, including:
    • High Gleason grade
    • Lymph node metastasis
    • Advanced tumor stage
    • High Ki67 labeling index
    • Positive surgical margin
    • Early biochemical recurrence[1]

Prognostic Significance

  • Reduced ANO7 expression is a strong and independent predictor of poor patient prognosis in prostate cancer[1].
  • The prognostic impact of ANO7 was independent of established pre- or postoperatively available parameters[1].

Potential Immune-Related Functions

While not directly described as an immune function, ANO7’s altered expression in prostate cancer may have implications for immune responses:

  • Low ANO7 expression was linked to TMPRSS2:ERG fusions, which are known to affect immune-related pathways in prostate cancer[1].
  • The loss of ANO7 expression in more aggressive prostate cancers could potentially affect immune cell interactions with tumor cells, though this is not explicitly stated in the provided information.

Molecular Associations

  • ANO7 expression changes were associated with specific molecular features of prostate cancer, including ERG status and PTEN deletions[1].

In summary, while ANO7 is not directly described as having a specific immune function, its altered expression in prostate cancer and its strong prognostic significance suggest it may play a role in the biology of prostate cancer that could indirectly affect immune responses. Further research would be needed to elucidate any direct immune functions of ANO7.

LDB1

Based on the search results, LDB1 (LIM Domain Binding 1) plays several important roles in immune function and hematopoiesis:

Erythroid Development and Globin Gene Regulation

LDB1 is crucial for β-globin activation and erythroid development:

  • It forms a complex with GATA-1, SCL, and LMO2 that is required for β-globin locus control region (LCR) and gene proximity[1].
  • LDB1 stabilizes this complex on β-globin chromatin, even though it’s not a DNA-binding component[1].
  • It’s necessary for enrichment of key transcriptional components like P-TEFb at the β-globin locus[1].

T-cell Development and Leukemia

LDB1 is involved in T-cell development and can contribute to leukemogenesis:

  • It’s required for Lmo2 oncogene-induced thymocyte self-renewal and neoplastic transformation[2].
  • Deletion of LDB1 corrects abnormal phenotypes and developmental defects in Lmo2 transgenic thymocytes[2].
  • LDB1 is part of a complex (Ldb1/Lmo2 complex) that imposes abnormal self-renewal on immature thymocytes, potentially leading to leukemogenesis[2].

Gene Expression Regulation

LDB1 controls gene expression at multiple levels:

  • It’s necessary for RNA polymerase II phosphorylation to the fully elongation-competent form at the β-globin gene[1].
  • LDB1 is required for the β-globin locus to migrate away from the nuclear periphery, which is necessary for robust transcription[1].
  • It regulates genes important for immune system processes, T-cell differentiation, activation, and signaling[2].

Hematopoietic Stem and Progenitor Cell (HSPC) Maintenance

LDB1 is critical for HSPC maintenance and self-renewal:

  • It binds to promoters and enhancers of genes known to regulate HSPC maintenance[2].
  • Some of these genes (e.g., Lyl1, Nfe2, Hhex) are also upregulated in preleukemic thymocytes and human early T-cell precursor acute lymphoblastic leukemia (ETP-ALL)[2].

In summary, LDB1 plays crucial roles in erythroid development, T-cell development, gene expression regulation, and hematopoietic stem cell maintenance. Its functions in these processes have significant implications for normal immune function as well as potential contributions to leukemogenesis.

TNFRSF6B

Based on the search results, TNFRSF6B (Tumor Necrosis Factor Receptor Superfamily Member 6B) plays several important roles in immune function:

  1. Regulation of immune responses:
  • TNFRSF6B is involved in the regulation of immune responses and immune system processes[1].
  • It affects the activity and differentiation of T lymphocytes, macrophages, and dendritic cells[1].
  1. Immune evasion:
  • TNFRSF6B acts as a decoy receptor that competitively binds to FasL, TL1A, and LIGHT expressed on T cells, blocking their ability to induce apoptosis and clear tumor cells[1][2].
  • This allows tumors to evade immune surveillance[2].
  1. Downregulation of immune responses:
  • TNFRSF6B can downregulate the immune system through several mechanisms[1]:
    • Inhibiting actin polymerization in T cells, reducing their interaction with antigen-presenting cells
    • Blocking activation of T cells by competing with LIGHT and TL1A
    • Inhibiting NF-kappa B activation, which is important for T cell activation
  1. Effects on specific immune cells:
  • TNFRSF6B expression is negatively correlated with CD8+ T cells, CD4+ memory T cells, and macrophages[1].
  • It is positively correlated with regulatory T cells (Tregs)[1].
  1. Interaction with immune checkpoints:
  • High TNFRSF6B expression is associated with decreased expression of immune checkpoint molecules like CTLA-4 and PD-1[1].
  1. Anti-apoptotic effects:
  • TNFRSF6B can protect cells against apoptosis, which may contribute to the persistence of inflammatory cells in chronic conditions[2][3].
  1. Role in chronic inflammation:
  • Overexpression of TNFRSF6B is associated with worsening of various chronic inflammatory diseases[3].

In summary, TNFRSF6B primarily functions as an immunosuppressive molecule that helps cells, particularly tumor cells, evade immune responses. It does this by interfering with T cell activation and function, promoting regulatory T cells, and inhibiting apoptosis of potentially harmful cells. Its expression is associated with poorer outcomes in various cancers and chronic inflammatory conditions.

MMP7

Based on the search results, MMP7 (Matrix Metalloproteinase 7) plays several important roles in immune function:

  1. Activation of antimicrobial peptides:
  • In the small intestine, MMP7 is responsible for activating α-defensins, which are broad-spectrum antimicrobial peptides produced by Paneth cells[1].
  1. Regulation of intestinal inflammation and barrier function:
  • MMP7 contributes to intestinal inflammation and increased intestinal permeability during endotoxemia[1].
  • It is involved in the degradation of tight junction proteins like Claudin-7, which impairs the intestinal epithelial barrier[2].
  1. Amplification of inflammatory responses:
  • MMP7-activated α-defensins can stimulate IL-6 release in macrophages and ileum explants, amplifying local inflammatory responses in the gut[1].
  1. Immune cell recruitment and activation:
  • MMP7 participates in neutrophil activation by modulating syndecan-1/CXCL1 complexes in airway epithelial cells[3].
  • It is involved in the shedding of Syndecan-4 from B-lymphocytes, which may influence chemokine influx and cell activation[3].
  1. Facilitation of immune cell access to the central nervous system:
  • In experimental autoimmune encephalomyelitis (EAE), MMP7 may facilitate immune cell access or re-stimulation in perivascular areas of the brain[4].
  1. Regulation of T cell responses:
  • MMP7-deficient mice show reduced T cell responses to myelin oligodendrocyte glycoprotein (MOG) in EAE models[4].
  1. Blood-brain barrier permeability:
  • Recombinant MMP7 can increase permeability between endothelial cells in an in vitro blood-brain barrier model[4].
  1. Tissue remodeling and repair:
  • MMP7 contributes to tissue remodeling and acute inflammation by degrading extracellular matrix proteins[3].
  1. Innate immune defense:
  • B-lymphocytes secrete MMP7 in response to microbial antigens like unmethylated CpG motifs (found in bacterial DNA) and β-glucans (found in fungal cell walls), suggesting a role in innate immune defense[3].

In summary, MMP7 plays diverse roles in immune function, including antimicrobial peptide activation, regulation of intestinal barrier function, amplification of inflammatory responses, immune cell recruitment and activation, and facilitation of immune cell access to the central nervous system. Its functions can be both protective (e.g., antimicrobial defense) and potentially harmful (e.g., contributing to excessive inflammation in certain conditions).

CCND1

Based on the search results, CCND1 (Cyclin D1) plays several important roles related to immune function and immunosuppression in cancer:

  1. Immunosuppression in the tumor microenvironment (TME):
  • CCND1 amplification is associated with various degrees of immune cell exclusion in the TME, including reduced infiltration of cytotoxic cells, T cells, CD8+ T cells, dendritic cells, and B cells.
  • It correlates with increased proportions of immunosuppressive cells like regulatory T cells (Tregs) and M2 macrophages.
  1. Impairment of anti-tumor immunity:
  • CCND1 amplification contributes to resistance to immune checkpoint inhibitor (ICI) therapy in multiple cancer types.
  • It is associated with poor clinical outcomes and inferior responses to ICI treatment.
  1. Regulation of immunosuppressive pathways:
  • CCND1 amplification correlates with activation of immunosuppressive signaling pathways, including TGF-β signaling, epithelial-mesenchymal transition, and hypoxia signaling.
  1. Modulation of angiogenesis and immune cell trafficking:
  • CCND1 may play a role in maintaining VEGF levels, which can suppress dendritic cell maturation and CD8+ T cell proliferation.
  • It may affect immune cell trafficking through modulation of adhesion molecules like ICAM1.
  1. Metabolic effects on immune function:
  • CCND1 amplification is associated with changes in oxidative phosphorylation and lipid metabolism pathways, which can impact immune cell function in the TME.
  1. Potential therapeutic implications:
  • The immunosuppressive effects of CCND1 amplification suggest that targeting CCND1 or related pathways (e.g., CDK4/6 inhibitors) may have potential in combination with immunotherapies.

In summary, while CCND1 is primarily known for its role in cell cycle regulation, its amplification appears to have significant immunosuppressive effects in the context of cancer. It contributes to an immunosuppressive tumor microenvironment, impairs anti-tumor immune responses, and may affect the efficacy of immunotherapies. These findings highlight CCND1 as an important factor in the intersection of cancer biology and immune function.

USP28

Based on the search results, USP28 (Ubiquitin Specific Peptidase 28) plays several important roles related to immune function:

  1. T cell regulation:
  • USP28 is crucial for T cell functionality and activation.
  • It regulates STAT5 phosphorylation in T cells.
  • USP28 deficiency results in early defects in T cell activation and functional changes.
  1. Cytokine production:
  • USP28 regulates IL-22 production in T lymphocytes.
  • USP28 knockout leads to reduced expression of IL-17.
  1. Intestinal inflammation:
  • USP28 exerts protective effects in acute intestinal inflammation.
  • USP28 knockout mice exhibit increased susceptibility to acute DSS-induced intestinal inflammation.
  1. T cell populations:
  • USP28 deficiency results in an increase in total T cells, mainly due to increased CD8+ T cell content.
  • It affects the balance of T cell subsets, including an increase in inducible regulatory T (iTreg) suppressive functions.
  1. Immune cell infiltration:
  • USP28 expression is associated with immune cell infiltration in various cancer types.
  • It correlates with infiltration levels of neutrophils and NK cells in most tumor types.
  1. Immune checkpoint regulation:
  • USP28 expression is correlated with immune checkpoint genes in several cancer types.
  • It may serve as a predictor of response to immune checkpoint inhibitor therapy, particularly anti-CTLA4 therapy in melanoma.
  1. Interaction with immune-related pathways:
  • USP28 is associated with various immune-related signaling pathways, including TNFA-signaling-via-NFKB, inflammatory response, and IL6-JAK-STAT3-signaling.

In summary, USP28 plays a significant role in T cell function, cytokine production, and regulation of intestinal inflammation. It also appears to be involved in broader aspects of immune regulation, particularly in the context of cancer, where it influences immune cell infiltration and may affect responses to immunotherapy.

RAB13

Based on the search results, RAB13 appears to have several important immune-related functions:

  1. Macrophage regulation:
  • RAB13 is involved in macrophage polarization, particularly in the context of sepsis. High RAB13 expression is associated with inhibition of M1-like macrophage polarization and promotion of M2-like polarization.
  1. Immune cell infiltration:
  • RAB13 expression is positively correlated with infiltration of macrophages and other immune cells in colorectal cancer.
  • It is associated with infiltration levels of neutrophils and NK cells in various tumor types.
  1. T cell regulation:
  • RAB13 expression is correlated with reduced infiltration of cytotoxic T cells, CD8+ T cells, and dendritic cells in the tumor microenvironment.
  • It is associated with increased proportions of immunosuppressive cells like regulatory T cells (Tregs).
  1. Cytokine signaling:
  • RAB13 is involved in cytokine signaling in the immune system, particularly in the context of inflammation and cancer.
  1. Tight junction regulation:
  • RAB13 regulates the structure and function of tight junctions, which are important for maintaining epithelial barriers that play a role in immune defense.
  1. Intracellular trafficking:
  • As a member of the Rab GTPase family, RAB13 is involved in regulating intracellular trafficking, which is crucial for various immune processes including antigen presentation and cytokine secretion.
  1. Sepsis severity:
  • High RAB13 expression is positively correlated with sepsis severity and negatively correlated with patient survival in sepsis.
  1. Potential therapeutic target:
  • RAB13’s involvement in immune cell infiltration and regulation suggests it could be a potential therapeutic target, particularly in cancer immunotherapy.

In summary, RAB13 appears to play significant roles in regulating various aspects of the immune response, particularly in the context of inflammation, sepsis, and cancer. Its functions include modulating macrophage polarization, influencing immune cell infiltration, and potentially contributing to immunosuppression in the tumor microenvironment.

MYO9B

Based on the search results, MYO9B (Myosin IXb) plays several important roles in immune function:

  1. T cell motility and tissue infiltration:
  • MYO9B enables CD8+ T cells to infiltrate nonlymphoid tissues by facilitating crossing of tissue barriers like basement membranes.
  • It is critical for T cell adaptation from lymphoid to nonlymphoid tissue surveillance.
  • MYO9B-deficient CD8+ T cells show impaired accumulation in nonlymphoid tissues and reduced control of skin infections.
  1. Regulation of Rho GTPase activity:
  • MYO9B acts as a negative regulator of the small GTPase Rho in T cells.
  • Lack of MYO9B leads to increased Rho-GTP levels and cell surface stiffness in T cells.
  1. Dendritic cell function:
  • MYO9B mutations are associated with altered dendritic cell functions.
  • It regulates phenotypic, metabolic, and functional maturation of dendritic cells.
  1. Autoimmune diabetes susceptibility:
  • Mutations in MYO9B are associated with increased susceptibility to autoimmune diabetes onset.
  • MYO9B knock-in or knockout in mice attenuates T cell-mediated autoimmune responses and inhibits autoimmune diabetes development.
  1. Regulation of immune cell morphology and motility:
  • MYO9B contributes to the regulation of morphology and motility in various immune cell types.
  1. Cytokine regulation:
  • MYO9B mutations affect levels of pro-inflammatory and anti-inflammatory cytokines in serum.
  1. Treg cell function:
  • MYO9B knockout or knock-in mice show increased regulatory T cell (Treg) proportion and function.

In summary, MYO9B plays crucial roles in T cell motility and tissue infiltration, dendritic cell function, regulation of autoimmune responses, and overall immune cell morphology and motility. Its functions are particularly important for adaptive immune responses and the establishment of protective tissue-resident T cell populations.

FLOT1

Based on the search results, FLOT1 (Flotillin-1) plays several important roles in immune function:

  1. T cell migration and motility:
  • FLOT1 accumulates in the uropod of polarized T cells, suggesting a role in T cell migration.
  • FLOT1-deficient CD8+ T cells showed alterations in cell shape and motility parameters in vivo, including reduced migration speeds and increased arrest coefficients.
  • However, the impact on overall T cell surveillance and function was relatively minor.
  1. Neutrophil and monocyte migration:
  • FLOT1-deficient mice show impaired recruitment of neutrophils and monocytes to inflammatory sites due to decreased migratory capacity.
  • FLOT1 deficiency in neutrophils leads to reduced myosin II activity and impaired cell migration.
  1. Lipid raft organization and signaling:
  • FLOT1 is a lipid raft-associated scaffolding protein that helps organize membrane microdomains.
  • It assembles at immunological synapses and may serve as a scaffold for T cell receptor signaling machinery.
  1. Endocytosis and membrane trafficking:
  • FLOT1 promotes endocytosis of various receptors and proteins involved in immune cell function.
  • It participates in membrane trafficking, which is important for immune cell processes.
  1. Cytoskeletal regulation:
  • FLOT1 is involved in modulating the actomyosin cytoskeleton of leukocytes, which is crucial for cell migration and function.
  1. T cell activation:
  • While FLOT1 is involved in T cell receptor recycling and signaling at the immunological synapse, its deficiency did not significantly impair T cell expansion or memory formation in the experimental conditions tested.
  1. Dendritic cell function:
  • FLOT1 may play a role in dendritic cell functions, though this was not extensively detailed in the provided information.

In summary, FLOT1 contributes to immune cell migration, membrane organization, and signaling processes, particularly in T cells and neutrophils. However, its impact on overall immune function appears to be relatively subtle under physiological conditions, as FLOT1-deficient T cells were still capable of mounting effective immune responses in the studies described.

PKNOX2

Based on the search results, PKNOX2 (PBX/knotted 1 homeobox 2) does not appear to have a direct immune function. However, it plays important roles that could indirectly affect immune responses, particularly in the context of cancer:

  1. Tumor suppressor function:
  • PKNOX2 acts as a tumor suppressor in lung cancer and gastric cancer.
  • It suppresses cancer cell proliferation, migration, and invasion.
  • PKNOX2 induces apoptosis and cell cycle arrest in cancer cells.
  1. Regulation of signaling pathways:
  • PKNOX2 inhibits the PI3K/AKT/mTOR signaling axis in lung cancer cells.
  • It activates the IGFBP5 and p53 signaling pathways in gastric cancer cells.
  1. Transcriptional regulation:
  • PKNOX2 binds to the promoter regions of IGFBP5 and TP53 genes and transcriptionally activates their expression.
  1. Fibrosis regulation:
  • In heart tissue, PKNOX2 has been identified as a novel regulator associated with physiological fibroblast activation.
  • It plays an inhibitory role in pathological fibrosis remodeling.
  1. Epigenetic regulation:
  • PKNOX2 is often silenced in cancers through promoter hypermethylation.
  • Its expression can be restored by DNA methyltransferase inhibitors.

While these functions are not directly related to the immune system, they could potentially influence immune responses in the tumor microenvironment or during tissue repair processes. For example, the regulation of fibrosis and cell proliferation could affect immune cell infiltration and function in tissues. However, the search results do not provide direct evidence of PKNOX2’s involvement in immune cell function or immune responses.

LGR4

Based on the search results, LGR4 (Leucine-rich repeat-containing G protein-coupled receptor 4) plays several important roles in immune function:

  1. Negative regulation of TLR2/4 signaling and innate immune responses:
  • LGR4 negatively modulates pattern-associated molecular pattern (PAMP)-induced immune responses.
  • Loss of LGR4 in macrophages leads to increased activation of NF-κB and overproduction of proinflammatory cytokines in response to TLR2/4 ligands[1].
  1. Attenuation of proinflammatory cytokine production:
  • LGR4 deficiency results in increased production of proinflammatory cytokines like TNF-α, IL-6, IL-1α, IFN-β, CXCL10, and GM-CSF in response to LPS stimulation[1].
  1. Regulation of macrophage polarization:
  • LGR4 promotes macrophage M2 polarization through Rspo/LGR4/Erk/Stat3 signaling[2].
  • LGR4 deficiency leads to fewer protumoral M2 tumor-associated macrophages (TAMs) in tumor microenvironments[2].
  1. Modulation of T cell responses:
  • LGR4 deficiency is associated with increased CD8+ T lymphocyte infiltration in tumor microenvironments[2].
  1. Anti-inflammatory effects:
  • LGR4 has been shown to protect different types of cells against inflammation, particularly in the digestive system[3].
  1. Regulation of NF-κB signaling:
  • LGR4 negatively regulates NF-κB activation in LPS-induced signaling pathways[1].
  1. Involvement in immune checkpoint therapy:
  • Blocking Rspo-LGR4 signaling can overcome resistance to anti-PD-1 therapy and improve the efficacy of PD-1 immunotherapy in cancer models[2].
  1. Participation in TGFβ and NF-κB signaling regulation:
  • LGR4 is involved in regulating TGFβ signaling in hematopoietic precursors and NF-κB signaling in intestinal cells[3].

In summary, LGR4 plays a crucial role in regulating innate immune responses, particularly by attenuating TLR2/4-mediated inflammation, modulating macrophage polarization, and influencing T cell responses. Its functions have significant implications for both normal immune homeostasis and cancer immunotherapy.

ITPK1

Based on the search results, ITPK1 (Inositol-Tetrakisphosphate 1-Kinase) plays several important roles in immune function:

  1. Regulation of T cell activation and development:
  • ITPK1 is involved in the Ca2+/NFAT signaling pathway, which helps limit the activity of T cells.
  • Deficiency in ITPK1 can lead to defects in T cell maturation and selection in the thymus.
  1. B cell survival and selection:
  • ITPK1 plays a crucial role in B cell survival and selection.
  • Knockout of ITPK1 in mice leads to inappropriate apoptosis of B lymphocytes and B cell anergy.
  1. Regulation of innate immune responses:
  • ITPK1 is involved in modulating innate immune responses, including neutrophil and mast cell activation.
  • It affects the function of innate immune cells by regulating calcium signaling and other intracellular pathways.
  1. Modulation of inflammatory responses:
  • ITPK1 helps regulate inflammatory responses by controlling the production of cytokines and other inflammatory mediators.
  1. Calcium signaling regulation:
  • ITPK1 is involved in the production of inositol 1,3,4,5-tetrakisphosphate (Ins(1,3,4,5)P4), which regulates calcium signaling in immune cells.
  • This regulation of calcium signaling is crucial for various immune cell functions, including activation, proliferation, and cytokine production.
  1. Potential role in autoimmune disorders:
  • Variations in ITPK1 have been associated with increased risk of autoimmune disorders like Kawasaki disease, suggesting a role in maintaining immune tolerance.
  1. Interaction with other signaling pathways:
  • ITPK1 interacts with other important signaling pathways in immune cells, such as the PI3K/AKT pathway, potentially influencing various aspects of immune cell function.

In summary, ITPK1 plays a critical role in regulating both innate and adaptive immune responses, primarily through its effects on calcium signaling, T and B cell development and function, and modulation of inflammatory responses. Its involvement in these processes makes it an important factor in maintaining proper immune function and preventing autoimmune disorders.

GDF7

Based on the search results, GDF7 (Growth Differentiation Factor 7) appears to have several important immune-related functions:

  1. Anti-inflammatory effects:
  • GDF7 prevents LPS-induced inflammatory responses in acute lung injury (ALI) models.
  • It inhibits inflammation and oxidative stress in ALI mice, protecting against pulmonary injury and dysfunction.
  1. Regulation of oxidative stress:
  • GDF7 treatment significantly reduces oxidative stress in LPS-induced ALI.
  1. Modulation of signaling pathways:
  • GDF7 activates AMP-activated protein kinase (AMPK), which is crucial for its anti-inflammatory and antioxidant effects.
  • It regulates the STING/AMPK pathway, downregulating the stimulator of interferon genes (STING) to activate AMPK.
  1. Potential therapeutic role:
  • GDF7 has been identified as a potential agent for the treatment of sepsis-induced ALI.
  1. Involvement in TGF-β superfamily signaling:
  • As a member of the TGF-β superfamily, GDF7 likely plays roles in development, differentiation, homeostasis, and disease processes related to immune function.
  1. Possible role in innate immunity:
  • While not directly stated, GDF7’s involvement in preventing LPS-induced responses suggests a role in innate immune regulation.
  1. Interaction with other immune-related factors:
  • GDF7 may interact with other members of the TGF-β superfamily that are known to regulate various aspects of immune function.

In summary, GDF7 appears to have significant anti-inflammatory and antioxidant properties, particularly in the context of acute lung injury and sepsis. Its ability to modulate key signaling pathways like AMPK and STING suggests a broader role in regulating immune responses and protecting against inflammatory damage.

TRIM24

The immune function of the gene TRIM24 (Tripartite Motif Containing 24) is significant in various aspects of immune response, particularly in antiviral immunity and T cell function:

  1. Antiviral Immunity:
    • TRIM24 acts as a positive regulator of antiviral signaling. It facilitates the activation of IRF3, a key transcription factor in the induction of type I interferons (IFN-I) during viral infections. This is crucial for mounting an effective antiviral response against RNA viruses, such as the vesicular stomatitis virus (VSV) [1][4].
    • Upon viral infection, TRIM24 translocates to the mitochondria, where it interacts with TRAF3 and mediates its K63-linked ubiquitination. This modification promotes TRAF3’s association with MAVS and TBK1, leading to the activation of downstream antiviral signaling pathways [1][4].
  2. Regulation of T Cell Responses:
    • TRIM24 is essential for T-helper 2 (TH2) cell-mediated immune responses, particularly in airway allergy and antihelminth immunity. TRIM24-deficient T cells exhibit reduced expression of IL-1 receptor (IL-1R), which impairs their activation in response to IL-1β, a critical cytokine for TH2 cell activation [2][3].
    • The absence of TRIM24 in T cells leads to diminished expression of various TH2 cytokines and chemokines, indicating its role in promoting TH2-associated immune functions [2].
  3. Feedback Regulation:
    • Interestingly, TRIM24 is also subject to transcriptional suppression by activated IRF3, forming a negative feedback loop that helps prevent overactivation of antiviral signaling [1]. This regulatory mechanism ensures a balanced immune response during viral infections.
  4. Impact on HIV-1 Transcription:
    • TRIM24 has been implicated in the regulation of latent HIV-1 expression by enhancing transcriptional elongation from the HIV-1 promoter in response to T-cell activation signals. This indicates its role in both HIV pathogenesis and T cell signaling [3].

In summary, TRIM24 plays a critical role in enhancing antiviral immunity through its regulation of interferon signaling and T cell responses. It is essential for effective TH2-mediated immunity and serves as a regulatory checkpoint to maintain balance in immune activation during viral infections.

ZDHHC7

The immune function of the gene ZDHHC7 (Zinc Finger DHHC-Type Containing 7) is primarily linked to its role in the regulation of the NLRP3 inflammasome, a critical component of the innate immune response:

  1. Palmitoylation of NLRP3:
    • ZDHHC7 is identified as a palmitoyl-acyltransferase that specifically palmitoylates the NLRP3 protein at cysteine residue Cys126. This post-translational modification is essential for NLRP3 activation in macrophages, which is crucial for the assembly and activation of the inflammasome.
  2. Inflammasome Activation:
    • The palmitoylation of NLRP3 by ZDHHC7 promotes its localization and oligomerization, which are necessary steps for recruiting the adaptor protein ASC (apoptosis-associated speck-like protein containing a CARD) and forming the inflammasome complex. This process ultimately leads to the activation of caspase-1 and the secretion of pro-inflammatory cytokines such as IL-1β and IL-18.
  3. Impact on Inflammation:
    • Disruption of ZDHHC7 function, through knockout or pharmacological inhibition, results in diminished NLRP3 activation, reduced cytokine secretion, and protection against inflammation-induced conditions such as LPS-induced endotoxic shock and monosodium urate-induced peritonitis in mouse models.
  4. Regulatory Mechanism:
    • The study highlights that ZDHHC7-mediated palmitoylation is vital for NLRP3’s resting localization on the trans-Golgi network (TGN) and its activation upon inflammatory stimuli. This suggests that ZDHHC7 plays a significant role in regulating inflammatory responses, particularly in macrophages.

In summary, ZDHHC7 functions as a critical regulator of the NLRP3 inflammasome through its role in palmitoylating NLRP3, thereby facilitating its activation and subsequent inflammatory responses. This makes ZDHHC7 a potential target for therapeutic strategies aimed at treating chronic inflammatory diseases associated with NLRP3 hyperactivation.

ARNT2

The immune function of the gene ARNT2 (Aryl Hydrocarbon Receptor Nuclear Translocator 2) is linked to its role in regulating immune responses, particularly in the context of cancer and inflammation:

  1. Regulation of Immune Cell Infiltration:
    • ARNT2 expression is correlated with the infiltration of various immune cells in nasopharyngeal carcinoma (NPC). High levels of ARNT2 are associated with increased infiltration of CD4+ T cells, macrophages, and dendritic cells, while negatively correlating with B cells and mast cells. This suggests that ARNT2 may influence the immune landscape within tumors, potentially affecting tumor progression and immune evasion [1].
  2. Interaction with Prostaglandin Pathways:
    • ARNT2 is implicated in the transcriptional regulation of PTGS2 (prostaglandin-endoperoxide synthase 2), an enzyme involved in the inflammatory response. The regulation of PTGS2 by ARNT2 may contribute to inflammatory processes and the modulation of immune responses in cancer [1].
  3. Inflammatory Response in Multiple Sclerosis:
    • In models of multiple sclerosis (MS), ARNT2 expression is altered, indicating its potential role in neuroinflammation. The expression levels of ARNT2 decline during peak disease stages, suggesting a relationship between ARNT2 and immune responses in the central nervous system [2].
  4. Potential Immunosuppressive Role:
    • The presence of resting dendritic cells, which are often associated with immunosuppressive states, was found to be higher in NPC samples with elevated ARNT2 expression. This indicates that ARNT2 may play a role in promoting an immunosuppressive microenvironment that could facilitate tumor progression [1].
  5. Regulatory Mechanisms:
    • As a transcription factor, ARNT2 regulates various target genes involved in immune responses. Its dysregulation can lead to alterations in immune signaling pathways, potentially impacting how immune cells respond to tumors or inflammatory stimuli [1][2].

In summary, ARNT2 appears to play a multifaceted role in immune function, particularly in cancer and inflammation. It influences immune cell infiltration, regulates inflammatory mediators like PTGS2, and may contribute to immunosuppressive environments within tumors. Further research could clarify its specific mechanisms and potential therapeutic implications in immune-related diseases.

RTEL1

The immune function of the gene RTEL1 (Regulator of Telomere Length 1) is primarily associated with its role in maintaining genomic stability, which indirectly impacts immune responses:

  1. Telomere Maintenance:
    • RTEL1 is essential for proper telomere replication and stability. It helps disassemble telomere loops (T-loops) and facilitates the replication of telomeres, preventing telomere shortening and dysfunction, which can lead to genomic instability and cellular senescence. This stability is crucial for the normal functioning of immune cells, particularly T cells and B cells, which require intact telomeres for proliferation and function [1][2].
  2. Immune Deficiencies:
    • Mutations in RTEL1 are linked to immunodeficiency conditions such as dyskeratosis congenita and aplastic anemia. Individuals with biallelic mutations in RTEL1 often exhibit short telomeres, leading to impaired hematopoiesis and increased susceptibility to infections due to compromised immune cell production [1]. This highlights RTEL1’s critical role in sustaining a functional immune system.
  3. Influence on Immune Cell Function:
    • The integrity of telomeres maintained by RTEL1 is vital for the lifespan and functionality of immune cells. Shortened telomeres can lead to premature senescence or apoptosis of T and B lymphocytes, thereby reducing the adaptive immune response [2].
  4. Regulation of RNA Processing:
    • RTEL1 also plays a role in RNA processing and trafficking, affecting the localization of certain RNAs within cells. This function may influence the expression of genes involved in immune responses, although specific mechanisms remain to be fully elucidated [2].
  5. Potential Role in Cancer Immunology:
    • Given that RTEL1 is involved in maintaining genomic integrity, its dysfunction can contribute to tumorigenesis. Tumors often exploit mechanisms to evade immune detection, and understanding how RTEL1 influences this process could provide insights into cancer immunotherapy strategies [2].

In summary, RTEL1 is crucial for maintaining telomere stability and genomic integrity, which are essential for proper immune cell function and overall immune health. Its mutations are associated with immunodeficiency syndromes, underscoring its importance in the immune system’s ability to respond effectively to pathogens.

IGF1R

The immune function of the gene IGF1R (Insulin-like Growth Factor 1 Receptor) is multifaceted, influencing various aspects of immune responses, particularly in the context of cancer and inflammation:

  1. Regulation of Immune Cell Activation:
    • IGF1R signaling plays a crucial role in the activation and proliferation of T cells. It enhances the proliferation and chemotaxis of activated T cells, indicating that IGF1R is important for T cell responses during immune activation [3][4].
  2. Influence on T Regulatory Cells:
    • IGF1R is involved in the modulation of T regulatory (Treg) cells, which are critical for maintaining immune tolerance and preventing autoimmunity. Activation of IGF1R promotes Treg proliferation and immunosuppressive functions, potentially contributing to an immunosuppressive tumor microenvironment [3][4].
  3. Impact on Tumor Immunology:
    • In the context of cancer, IGF1R promotes an immunosuppressive environment by enhancing the recruitment and activity of immunosuppressive cells such as M2 macrophages and myeloid-derived suppressor cells (MDSCs). This can impair anti-tumor immunity by inhibiting the activation of cytotoxic CD8+ T cells and natural killer (NK) cells [3][4].
  4. CNS Inflammation Modulation:
    • IGF1R signaling in central nervous system (CNS) macrophages regulates their morphology and function, influencing autoimmune CNS inflammation. Deletion of IGF1R in these cells results in increased severity of autoimmune conditions, suggesting a protective role against inflammation [2].
  5. Cytokine Production:
    • IGF1R influences cytokine production by immune cells, which can modulate inflammatory responses. For example, it has been shown to affect the production of IL-10, an anti-inflammatory cytokine, indicating its role in balancing immune responses [1][3].
  6. Tumor Microenvironment Dynamics:
    • In tumor models, IGF1R deficiency leads to reduced tumor growth and inflammation, as well as increased infiltration of effector T cells. This suggests that IGF1R signaling contributes to maintaining an immunosuppressive tumor microenvironment that favors tumor progression [4].

In summary, IGF1R plays a significant role in regulating immune responses through its effects on T cell activation, modulation of Treg function, and influence on the tumor microenvironment. Its signaling pathways are critical for both promoting and inhibiting various aspects of immunity, particularly in cancer contexts where it can contribute to immune evasion and tumor progression.

WTAP

The immune function of the gene WTAP (Wilms Tumor 1-Associated Protein) is multifaceted, particularly in the context of inflammation and cancer:

  1. Role in m6A RNA Methylation:
    • WTAP is a key component of the m6A methyltransferase complex, which adds N6-methyladenosine (m6A) modifications to RNA. This modification influences various biological processes, including gene expression regulation and RNA stability, which are crucial for immune responses.
  2. Regulation of Inflammatory Responses:
    • WTAP expression is significantly upregulated in response to inflammatory stimuli, such as lipopolysaccharides (LPS) and cytokines. It enhances the synthesis of pro-inflammatory cytokines by facilitating the m6A modification of their mRNA, thereby promoting inflammation. This suggests that WTAP plays a role in amplifying inflammatory responses during immune activation.
  3. Impact on Immune Cell Infiltration:
    • High levels of WTAP expression correlate with increased infiltration of various immune cells, including CD4+ T cells, CD8+ T cells, macrophages, and dendritic cells in tumors. This indicates that WTAP may influence the tumor microenvironment by modulating immune cell dynamics and potentially affecting tumor progression.
  4. Antiviral Response Regulation:
    • WTAP is involved in antiviral immunity by maintaining the expression of key components in the type I interferon signaling pathway. During viral infections, its degradation can restrict antiviral responses, highlighting its dual role in immune regulation.
  5. Prognostic Marker in Cancer:
    • In various cancers, including hepatocellular carcinoma (HCC) and esophageal carcinoma (ESCA), WTAP has been identified as a prognostic marker associated with poor outcomes. Its expression levels are linked to immune cell infiltration patterns, suggesting that it may serve as a biomarker for tumor immunogenicity.
  6. Potential Therapeutic Target:
    • Given its role in regulating inflammatory responses and immune cell dynamics, targeting WTAP could be a potential therapeutic strategy for managing excessive inflammation or enhancing anti-tumor immunity.

In summary, WTAP plays a significant role in regulating immune responses through its involvement in m6A RNA methylation, modulation of inflammatory signaling pathways, and influencing immune cell infiltration within tumors. Its expression levels have implications for cancer prognosis and therapeutic strategies aimed at modulating immune responses.

STXBP1

The immune function of the gene STXBP1 (Syntaxin Binding Protein 1) is primarily associated with its role in regulating immune cell cytotoxicity, particularly in natural killer (NK) cells and cytotoxic T cells:

  1. Regulation of Cytotoxic Activity:
    • STXBP1 is crucial for the granule exocytosis pathway in NK and CD8+ T cells. It has been shown to be responsible for approximately 50% of the cytotoxic activity of these immune cells when stimulated with interleukin-2 (IL-2) [4]. This suggests that STXBP1 plays a significant role in the ability of these cells to kill target cells effectively.
  2. Granule Exocytosis:
    • The protein is involved in the docking and fusion of cytotoxic granules with the plasma membrane of immune cells, which is essential for releasing perforin and granzymes—key molecules that mediate cell lysis [4]. This process is vital for the immune response against infected or malignant cells.
  3. Functional Redundancy with Other Proteins:
    • While STXBP1 contributes significantly to NK and T cell function, it operates alongside other proteins like STXBP2. Although there may be some redundancy, STXBP1 has distinct roles that are critical for effective immune responses [4].
  4. Mast Cell Function:
    • Although STXBP1 was investigated in the context of mast cell activation, studies indicate that it is not required for IgE-mediated mast cell degranulation or allergic inflammation [1]. This suggests that while STXBP1 plays a role in some immune cells, its functions can vary widely across different cell types.
  5. Implications in Immune Disorders:
    • Given its critical role in cytotoxicity, mutations or deficiencies in STXBP1 can lead to impaired immune responses, which may contribute to increased susceptibility to infections or malignancies.

In summary, STXBP1 is essential for the cytotoxic functions of NK and CD8+ T cells by facilitating granule exocytosis. Its role underscores the importance of precise regulation in immune cell functionality, particularly in the context of adaptive immunity and responses to pathogens or tumors.

ZNF395

The immune function of the gene ZNF395 (Zinc Finger Protein 395) is primarily associated with its role in regulating the expression of interferon-stimulated genes (ISGs) and pro-inflammatory cytokines, particularly in the context of antiviral responses and inflammation:

  1. Activation of Interferon-Stimulated Genes:
    • ZNF395 acts as a transcription factor that enhances the expression of a subset of ISGs, including chemokines such as CXCL10 and CXCL11. These chemokines are critical for attracting immune cells to sites of infection or inflammation, thereby playing a vital role in the antiviral immune response [1][2].
  2. Independence from Interferon:
    • While ZNF395 enhances the expression of ISGs, it operates independently of interferon signaling pathways. It can amplify the effects of interferon on ISG expression, indicating its importance in modulating the innate immune response [1].
  3. Regulation by IKK Signaling:
    • The transcriptional activity of ZNF395 is regulated by IκB kinase (IKK) signaling, which is necessary for its activation and also promotes its degradation. This regulation ensures that ZNF395’s activity is tightly controlled, allowing for a balanced immune response [2][4].
  4. Role in Hypoxia-Induced Inflammation:
    • ZNF395 is implicated in hypoxia-associated inflammation by superactivating pro-inflammatory cytokines such as IL-1β, IL-6, and IL-8 under low oxygen conditions. This suggests that ZNF395 may contribute to inflammatory processes in tumor microenvironments, linking it to both immune responses and cancer progression [3][4].
  5. Impact on Immune Cell Function:
    • In studies involving glioma, ZNF395 has been shown to influence macrophage polarization, promoting M2 macrophage characteristics that are associated with tumor progression and immunosuppression. This highlights its role in shaping the immune microenvironment in cancer [3].
  6. Antiviral Response:
    • ZNF395 has been found to be downregulated during high viral loads (e.g., HIV and CMV), suggesting that it may play a protective role in antiviral immunity by regulating the expression of genes involved in combating viral infections [1].

In summary, ZNF395 is an important regulator of the innate immune response, particularly through its activation of ISGs and pro-inflammatory cytokines. Its roles extend to influencing macrophage polarization and modulating responses to hypoxia, making it a significant factor in both immune regulation and cancer biology.

SEPT2

The immune function of the gene SEPT2 (Septin 2) is primarily associated with its role in the cytoskeleton and its involvement in immune responses, particularly in phagocytosis and host defense mechanisms:

  1. Cytoskeletal Role:
    • SEPT2 is part of the septin family of proteins, which are known to contribute to the organization of the cytoskeleton. This structural role is crucial for various cellular processes, including cytokinesis, cell motility, and the formation of membrane structures.
  2. Phagocytosis:
    • SEPT2 has been shown to play a significant role in phagocytosis, a process by which immune cells engulf and eliminate pathogens. Studies indicate that silencing SEPT2 can inhibit antibody-mediated phagocytosis by 50-70%, highlighting its importance in the function of phagocytic cells like macrophages and neutrophils.
  3. Host Defense Against Infections:
    • Septins, including SEPT2, are implicated in the host’s defense against various pathogens, including bacteria and viruses. They may participate in forming structures around intracellular pathogens, thereby aiding in their degradation and preventing their replication.
  4. Regulation of Immune Responses:
    • SEPT2 is involved in the recruitment of multiprotein complexes necessary for the formation of phagosomes, which are essential for processing and presenting antigens to T cells. This process is vital for initiating adaptive immune responses.
  5. Pathogen Sensing:
    • The septin cytoskeleton can sense pathogenic microbes and trigger host defense mechanisms. This includes rearranging to form protective structures that can sequester pathogens within immune cells.
  6. Involvement in Inflammation:
    • By participating in the organization of immune cell structures and facilitating phagocytosis, SEPT2 indirectly influences inflammatory responses, which are critical for combating infections.

In summary, SEPT2 plays a crucial role in immune function by supporting phagocytosis, contributing to host defense mechanisms against infections, and participating in the organization of cytoskeletal structures necessary for effective immune responses. Its involvement in these processes underscores the importance of septins in maintaining immune system integrity and functionality.

TGFA

The immune function of the gene TGFA (Transforming Growth Factor Alpha) is primarily linked to its role as a cytokine that influences various immune responses, particularly in the context of inflammation and tissue repair:

  1. Cytokine Activity:
    • TGFA is a member of the transforming growth factor (TGF) family and functions similarly to TGF-β in regulating immune responses. It plays a role in promoting cellular proliferation, differentiation, and survival, particularly in epithelial cells and some immune cells.
  2. Regulation of Immune Cell Function:
    • TGFA has been shown to influence the behavior of various immune cells, including T cells and macrophages. It can modulate T cell activation and differentiation, potentially impacting the balance between pro-inflammatory and anti-inflammatory responses.
  3. Promotion of Inflammation:
    • TGFA is involved in inflammatory processes, acting as a pro-inflammatory cytokine that can enhance the recruitment and activation of immune cells at sites of injury or infection. This function is critical for initiating and sustaining immune responses.
  4. Influence on Tumor Microenvironment:
    • In cancer, TGFA can contribute to the creation of an immunosuppressive tumor microenvironment by promoting the differentiation of regulatory T cells (Tregs) and inhibiting cytotoxic T cell functions. This can facilitate tumor growth and metastasis by evading immune surveillance.
  5. Role in Tissue Repair:
    • Beyond its immune functions, TGFA is also involved in tissue repair processes. It can promote wound healing by stimulating cell proliferation and migration, which are essential for tissue regeneration following injury.
  6. Interaction with Other Cytokines:
    • TGFA may interact with other cytokines and signaling pathways to fine-tune immune responses. For instance, it can cooperate with TGF-β to regulate various aspects of immunity, including the differentiation of B cells and the production of immunoglobulins.

In summary, TGFA plays a significant role in modulating immune responses through its effects on inflammation, immune cell function, and tissue repair. Its dual role as both a promoter of inflammation and a contributor to immunosuppression in tumors highlights its importance in maintaining immune balance under different physiological conditions.

CDCA7L

The immune function of the gene CDCA7L (Cell Division Cycle Associated 7 Like) is primarily associated with its role in regulating immune responses, particularly in the context of cancer and lymphoid malignancies:

  1. Regulation of Immune Cell Function:
    • CDCA7L has been implicated in the modulation of immune cell activities, particularly in lymphocytes. It may influence T cell apoptosis, which is crucial for maintaining immune homeostasis and preventing autoimmunity. This suggests a potential role in regulating the survival and function of T cells during immune responses.
  2. Association with Tumor Immunity:
    • In various cancers, including lymphomas, CDCA7L is overexpressed, indicating its involvement in tumorigenesis. Its expression levels are correlated with the proliferation of tumor cells, which can impact the immune landscape within tumors. High expression may contribute to an immunosuppressive environment that favors tumor growth.
  3. Impact on Immune Infiltration:
    • The expression of CDCA7L is associated with changes in immune cell infiltration within tumors. For instance, lower methylation levels of related genes have been linked to increased immune cell infiltration, suggesting that CDCA7L may play a role in shaping the tumor microenvironment and influencing how immune cells interact with cancer cells.
  4. Potential as a Therapeutic Target:
    • Given its role in lymphomagenesis and tumor progression, CDCA7L could be considered a potential target for therapeutic interventions aimed at enhancing anti-tumor immunity or inhibiting tumor growth.
  5. Cytoskeletal Dynamics:
    • CDCA7L is involved in cytoskeletal dynamics, which are essential for processes like cell migration and invasion. This function can impact how immune cells move and respond to signals within the tumor microenvironment.

In summary, CDCA7L plays a significant role in regulating immune responses, particularly through its effects on T cell function and its involvement in cancer biology. Its expression influences immune cell dynamics and could serve as a potential target for therapies aimed at modulating immune responses in cancer. Further research is needed to fully elucidate its mechanisms and implications in various immune contexts.

EGF

The immune function of the gene EGF (Epidermal Growth Factor) is primarily related to its role as a signaling molecule that influences various immune processes, particularly in inflammation and tissue repair:

  1. Cytokine Activity:
    • EGF is a potent mitogen that stimulates cell proliferation, differentiation, and survival. It plays a crucial role in wound healing and tissue regeneration, which are essential processes in immune responses following injury or infection.
  2. Regulation of Inflammatory Responses:
    • EGF can modulate inflammatory responses by influencing the activity of immune cells. It has been shown to promote the activation and migration of macrophages, which are key players in the immune system responsible for phagocytosing pathogens and secreting pro-inflammatory cytokines.
  3. Impact on Dendritic Cells:
    • EGF affects dendritic cell function, enhancing their ability to present antigens and activate T cells. This interaction is critical for initiating adaptive immune responses, linking innate and adaptive immunity.
  4. Role in Tumor Immunology:
    • In the context of cancer, EGF signaling can contribute to an immunosuppressive tumor microenvironment. Tumors often exploit EGF pathways to evade immune detection, promoting the proliferation of tumor cells while inhibiting effective anti-tumor immune responses.
  5. Interaction with Other Cytokines:
    • EGF can interact with other cytokines and growth factors to modulate immune responses further. For example, it may work in conjunction with transforming growth factor-beta (TGF-β) to regulate the balance between pro-inflammatory and anti-inflammatory signals.
  6. Potential Therapeutic Target:
    • Given its role in inflammation and tissue repair, EGF signaling pathways are being explored as potential therapeutic targets for various inflammatory diseases and cancer treatments.

In summary, EGF plays a significant role in regulating immune functions through its effects on inflammation, cell proliferation, and interactions with various immune cells. Its involvement in both tissue repair and tumor biology highlights its importance in maintaining immune homeostasis and responding to pathological conditions.

AGPAT3

The immune function of the gene AGPAT3 (1-Acylglycerol-3-Phosphate O-Acyltransferase 3) is associated with its role in lipid metabolism and its implications in immune responses, particularly in the context of inflammation and cancer:

  1. Lipid Metabolism:
    • AGPAT3 is involved in the synthesis of phosphatidic acid (PA) from lysophosphatidic acid (LPA), which is a key lipid mediator that influences various cellular processes, including cell proliferation, migration, and survival. This metabolic pathway is crucial for maintaining cellular homeostasis and supporting immune cell functions.
  2. Regulation of Inflammatory Responses:
    • The products of AGPAT3 activity, particularly PA and LPA, are known to play significant roles in regulating inflammatory responses. LPA can act on its receptors to promote macrophage activation and polarization, influencing the overall inflammatory environment.
  3. Impact on Immune Cell Function:
    • AGPAT3 has been shown to favor the synthesis of polyunsaturated fatty acid (PUFA)-containing phospholipids, which are important for maintaining membrane integrity and fluidity in immune cells. This can affect the functionality of T cells and macrophages during immune responses.
  4. Cancer Immunology:
    • In cancer contexts, AGPAT3 may influence tumor progression and the immune microenvironment. For instance, its activity can affect the infiltration and activation of immune cells within tumors, potentially impacting anti-tumor immunity. Changes in lipid metabolism mediated by AGPAT3 can alter how immune cells interact with tumor cells.
  5. Potential as a Therapeutic Target:
    • Given its role in lipid metabolism and inflammation, AGPAT3 may represent a potential target for therapeutic interventions aimed at modulating immune responses in inflammatory diseases or cancer.
  6. Tissue-Specific Functions:
    • AGPAT3’s functions are also tissue-specific; for example, it plays a role in the synthesis of DHA-containing phospholipids in the brain and eyes, which may have implications for neuroinflammation and retinal health.

In summary, AGPAT3 is involved in lipid metabolism with significant implications for immune function through its regulation of inflammatory processes and interactions with immune cells. Its role in cancer biology further underscores its importance in modulating the immune landscape within tumors.
